<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Mars</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the planet. For the deity, see <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars (mythology)</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Mars_(disambiguation)" title="Mars (disambiguation)">Mars (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="plainlist">
<ul><li>95.97% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a></li>
<li>1.93% <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a></li>
<li>1.89% <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a></li>
<li>0.146% <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a></li>
<li>0.0557% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Mars</b> is the fourth <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the second-smallest planet in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> after <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>. In English, Mars carries a name of the <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Roman god of war</a>, and is often referred to as the "<b>Red Planet</b>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zubrin1997_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zubrin1997-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees2012_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rees2012-17">[15]</a></sup> because the reddish <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron oxide</a> prevalent on its surface gives it a <a href="/wiki/Mars_surface_color" title="Mars surface color">reddish appearance</a> that is distinctive among the astronomical bodies visible to the naked eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_hematite_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_hematite-18">[16]</a></sup> Mars is a <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planet</a> with a thin <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, having surface features reminiscent both of the <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and the valleys, deserts, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_ice_caps" title="Polar ice caps">polar ice caps</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_period" title="Rotational period">rotational period</a> and seasonal cycles of Mars are likewise similar to those of Earth, as is the tilt that produces the seasons. Mars is the site of <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a>, the largest <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcano</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_mountains_in_the_Solar_System" title="List of tallest mountains in the Solar System">second-highest known mountain in the Solar System</a>, and of <a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">Valles Marineris</a>, one of the largest canyons in the Solar System. The smooth <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borealis_basin" title="Borealis basin">Borealis basin</a> in the northern hemisphere covers 40% of the planet and may be a giant impact feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-northcratersn_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-northcratersn-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-northcraterguard_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-northcraterguard-20">[18]</a></sup> Mars has two <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars" title="Moons of Mars">moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a>, which are small and irregularly shaped. These may be captured <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adler_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-adler-22">[20]</a></sup> similar to <a href="/wiki/5261_Eureka" title="5261 Eureka">5261 Eureka</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mars_trojan" title="Mars trojan">Mars trojan</a>.
</p><p>There are ongoing investigations assessing the past <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitability</a> potential of Mars, as well as <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">the possibility of extant life</a>. Future astrobiology missions are planned, including the <a href="/wiki/Mars_2020" title="Mars 2020">Mars 2020</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ExoMars_rover" title="ExoMars rover">ExoMars</a> rovers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curiosity_search_for_life_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-curiosity_search_for_life-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MER_Search_for_Life_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-MER_Search_for_Life-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExoMars's_search_for_life_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-ExoMars's_search_for_life-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_2020_search_for_life_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_2020_search_for_life-26">[24]</a></sup> Liquid <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">water</a> cannot exist on the surface of Mars due to low atmospheric pressure, which is less than 1% of the Earth's,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov-27">[25]</a></sup> except at the lowest elevations for short periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-h-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jgr110_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-jgr110-29">[27]</a></sup> The two polar ice caps appear to be made largely of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kostama_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-kostama-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci299_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci299-31">[29]</a></sup> The volume of water ice in the south polar ice cap, if melted, would be sufficient to cover the entire planetary surface to a depth of 11 meters (36 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa070315_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa070315-32">[30]</a></sup> In November 2016, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> reported finding a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">underground ice</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Utopia_Planitia" title="Utopia Planitia">Utopia Planitia</a> region of Mars. The volume of water detected has been estimated to be equivalent to the volume of water in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Lake Superior</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20161122_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20161122-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mars can easily be seen from Earth with the naked eye, as can its reddish coloring. Its <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> reaches −2.94,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-12">[10]</a></sup> which is surpassed only by <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, the Moon, and the Sun. Optical ground-based telescopes are typically limited to resolving features about 300 kilometers (190 mi) across when Earth and Mars are closest because of Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usra_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-usra-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<p>Mars is approximately half the diameter of Earth with a surface area only slightly less than the total area of Earth's dry land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-10">[8]</a></sup> Mars is less dense than Earth, having about 15% of Earth's volume and 11% of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, resulting in about 38% of Earth's surface gravity. The red-orange appearance of the Martian surface is caused by <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a>, or rust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rust_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-rust-37">[35]</a></sup> It can look like butterscotch;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ismars_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ismars-38">[36]</a></sup> other common surface colors include golden, brown, tan, and greenish, depending on the <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ismars_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-ismars-38">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:586px;max-width:586px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:176px;max-width:176px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1562" data-file-width="2399" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mars%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/174px-Mars%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mars%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/261px-Mars%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mars%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/348px-Mars%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="174"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Comparison: <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and Mars</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:200px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Mars.ogv/854px--Mars.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="40.033333333333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Mars.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/dd/Mars.ogv/Mars.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>
<p>Like Earth, Mars has <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiated</a> into a dense metallic <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a> overlaid by less dense materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo_2005_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nimmo_2005-39">[37]</a></sup> Current models of its interior imply a core with a radius of about 1,794 ± 65 kilometers (1,115 ± 40 mi), consisting primarily of <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93nickel_alloy" title="Iron–nickel alloy">iron and nickel</a> with about 16–17% <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus213_2_451_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus213_2_451-40">[38]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfide" title="Iron(II) sulfide">iron(II) sulfide</a> core is thought to be twice as rich in lighter elements as Earth's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacque03_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacque03-41">[39]</a></sup> The core is surrounded by a silicate <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> that formed many of the <a href="/wiki/Tectonics_of_Mars" title="Tectonics of Mars">tectonic</a> and volcanic features on the planet, but it appears to be dormant. Besides silicon and oxygen, the most abundant elements in the Martian <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> are iron, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>. The average thickness of the planet's crust is about 50 km (31 mi), with a maximum thickness of 125 km (78 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacque03_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-jacque03-41">[39]</a></sup> Earth's crust averages 40 km (25 mi).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_geology">Surface geology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">Geology of Mars</a></div>
<p>Mars is a <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planet</a> that consists of minerals containing <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, and other elements that typically make up <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a>. The surface of Mars is primarily composed of <a href="/wiki/Tholeiitic_magma_series" title="Tholeiitic magma series">tholeiitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science324_5928_736_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-science324_5928_736-42">[40]</a></sup> although parts are more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>-rich than typical basalt and may be similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andesitic" title="Andesitic">andesitic</a> rocks on Earth or silica glass. Regions of low <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> suggest concentrations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plagioclase_feldspar" title="Plagioclase feldspar">plagioclase feldspar</a>, with northern low albedo regions displaying higher than normal concentrations of sheet silicates and high-silicon glass. Parts of the southern highlands include detectable amounts of high-calcium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyroxenes" title="Pyroxenes">pyroxenes</a>. Localized concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> have been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jgr107_E6_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-jgr107_E6-43">[41]</a></sup> Much of the surface is deeply covered by finely grained <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a> dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci300a_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci300a-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci300b_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci300b-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4249" data-file-width="3773" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png/220px-USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png/330px-USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png/440px-USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USGS-MarsMap-sim3292-20140714-crop.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">Geologic map</a> of Mars (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USGS" title="USGS">USGS</a>, 2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-20140714_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-20140714-46">[44]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Although Mars has no evidence of a structured global <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magnetosphere_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-magnetosphere-47">[45]</a></sup> observations show that parts of the planet's crust have been magnetized, suggesting that alternating polarity reversals of its dipole field have occurred in the past. This <a href="/wiki/Paleomagnetism" title="Paleomagnetism">paleomagnetism</a> of magnetically susceptible minerals is similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_striping" title="Magnetic striping">alternating bands found on Earth's ocean floors</a>. One theory, published in 1999 and re-examined in October 2005 (with the help of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a></i>), is that these bands suggest <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonic activity</a> on Mars four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> years ago, before the planetary <a href="/wiki/Dynamo_theory" title="Dynamo theory">dynamo</a> ceased to function and the planet's magnetic field faded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plates_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-plates-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is thought that, during the <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Solar System's formation</a>, Mars was created as the result of a <a href="/wiki/Stochastic_process" title="Stochastic process">stochastic process</a> of run-away accretion of material from the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disk</a> that orbited the Sun. Mars has many distinctive chemical features caused by its position in the Solar System. Elements with comparatively low boiling points, such as <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphur" title="Sulphur">sulphur</a>, are much more common on Mars than Earth; these elements were probably pushed outward by the young Sun's energetic <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr96_1_4_197_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssr96_1_4_197-49">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the formation of the planets, all were subjected to the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a>". About 60% of the surface of Mars shows a record of impacts from that era,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zharkov93_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-zharkov93-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus165_1_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus165_1-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barlow88_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-barlow88-52">[50]</a></sup> whereas much of the remaining surface is probably underlain by immense impact basins caused by those events. There is evidence of an enormous impact basin in the northern hemisphere of Mars, spanning 10,600 by 8,500 km (6,600 by 5,300 mi), or roughly four times the size of the Moon's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Pole_%E2%80%93_Aitken_basin" title="South Pole – Aitken basin">South Pole – Aitken basin</a>, the largest impact basin yet discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-northcratersn_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-northcratersn-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-northcraterguard_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-northcraterguard-20">[18]</a></sup> This theory suggests that Mars was struck by a <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>-sized body about four billion years ago. The event, thought to be the cause of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martian_hemispheric_dichotomy" title="Martian hemispheric dichotomy">Martian hemispheric dichotomy</a>, created the smooth <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borealis_basin" title="Borealis basin">Borealis basin</a> that covers 40% of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam080627_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam080627-53">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt080626_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt080626-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4717" data-file-width="7683" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg/220px-Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg/330px-Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg/440px-Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eso1509a_-_Mars_planet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of how Mars may have looked four billion years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The geological history of Mars can be split into many periods, but the following are the three primary periods:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jog91_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-jog91-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr_96_1_4_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssr_96_1_4-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Noachian" title="Noachian">Noachian</a> period</b> (named after <a href="/wiki/Noachis_Terra" title="Noachis Terra">Noachis Terra</a>): Formation of the oldest extant surfaces of Mars, 4.5 to 3.5 billion years ago. Noachian age surfaces are scarred by many large impact craters. The <a href="/wiki/Tharsis" title="Tharsis">Tharsis</a> bulge, a volcanic upland, is thought to have formed during this period, with extensive flooding by liquid water late in the period.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Hesperian" title="Hesperian">Hesperian</a> period</b> (named after <a href="/wiki/Hesperia_Planum" title="Hesperia Planum">Hesperia Planum</a>): 3.5 to between 3.3 and 2.9 billion years ago. The Hesperian period is marked by the formation of extensive lava plains.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Amazonian_(Mars)" title="Amazonian (Mars)">Amazonian period</a></b> (named after <a href="/wiki/Amazonis_Planitia" title="Amazonis Planitia">Amazonis Planitia</a>): between 3.3 and 2.9 billion years ago to the present. Amazonian regions have few <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">meteorite impact</a> craters, but are otherwise quite varied. <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a> formed during this period, with lava flows elsewhere on Mars.</li></ul>
<p>Geological activity is still taking place on Mars. The <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_Valles" title="Athabasca Valles">Athabasca Valles</a> is home to sheet-like lava flows created about 200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">Mya</a>. Water flows in the <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">grabens</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Cerberus_Fossae" title="Cerberus Fossae">Cerberus Fossae</a> occurred less than 20 Mya, indicating equally recent volcanic intrusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag44_4_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-ag44_4-58">[56]</a></sup> On February 19, 2008, images from the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a></i> showed evidence of an avalanche from a 700-metre-high (2,300 ft) cliff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dc080304_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-dc080304-59">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil">Soil</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Martian_soil" title="Martian soil">Martian soil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg/220px-Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg/330px-Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg/440px-Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spirit_Mars_Silica_April_20_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exposure of silica-rich dust uncovered by the <i><a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)">Spirit</a></i> rover</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)">Phoenix</a></i> lander returned data showing Martian soil to be slightly alkaline and containing elements such as <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>. These nutrients are found in soils on Earth, and they are necessary for growth of plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc080627_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc080627-60">[58]</a></sup> Experiments performed by the lander showed that the Martian soil has a <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a> <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of 7.7, and contains 0.6% of the <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salt</a> <a href="/wiki/Perchlorate" title="Perchlorate">perchlorate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marssalt_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-marssalt-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_soil_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_soil-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dark_slope_streak" title="Dark slope streak">Streaks</a> are common across Mars and new ones appear frequently on steep slopes of craters, troughs, and valleys. The streaks are dark at first and get lighter with age. The streaks can start in a tiny area, then spread out for hundreds of metres. They have been seen to follow the edges of boulders and other obstacles in their path. The commonly accepted theories include that they are dark underlying layers of soil revealed after avalanches of bright dust or <a href="/wiki/Dust_devil" title="Dust devil">dust devils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl_dust_devil_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpl_dust_devil-65">[63]</a></sup> Several other explanations have been put forward, including those that involve water or even the growth of organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gpl29_23_41_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-gpl29_23_41-66">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oleb33_4_515_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-oleb33_4_515-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrology">Hydrology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">Water on Mars</a></div>
<p>Liquid water cannot exist on the surface of Mars due to low atmospheric pressure, which is less than 1% that of Earth's,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gov_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov-27">[25]</a></sup> except at the lowest elevations for short periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-h-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jgr110_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-jgr110-29">[27]</a></sup> The two polar ice caps appear to be made largely of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kostama_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-kostama-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci299_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-sci299-31">[29]</a></sup> The volume of water ice in the south polar ice cap, if melted, would be sufficient to cover the entire planetary surface to a depth of 11 meters (36 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa070315_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa070315-32">[30]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> mantle stretches from the pole to latitudes of about 60°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kostama_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-kostama-30">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Water_on_terrestrial_planets_of_the_Solar_System" title="Water on terrestrial planets of the Solar System">Large quantities of water ice</a> are thought to be trapped within the thick <a href="/wiki/Cryosphere" title="Cryosphere">cryosphere</a> of Mars. Radar data from <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a></i> show large quantities of water ice at both poles (July 2005)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-specials1_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-specials1-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc040124_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc040124-69">[67]</a></sup> and at middle latitudes (November 2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsg.utexas.edu_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-jsg.utexas.edu-70">[68]</a></sup> The Phoenix lander directly sampled water ice in shallow Martian soil on July 31, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacecraft1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-spacecraft1-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="431" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg/220px-Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg/330px-Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nasa_mars_opportunity_rock_water_150_eng_02mar04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photomicrograph by <i><a href="/wiki/Opportunity_(rover)" title="Opportunity (rover)">Opportunity</a></i> showing a gray <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> <a href="/wiki/Concretion" title="Concretion">concretion</a>, nicknamed "blueberries", indicative of the past existence of liquid water</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">Landforms</a> visible on Mars strongly suggest that liquid water has existed on the planet's surface. Huge linear swathes of scoured ground, known as <a href="/wiki/Outflow_channels" title="Outflow channels">outflow channels</a>, cut across the surface in about 25 places. These are thought to be a record of erosion caused by the catastrophic release of water from subsurface aquifers, though some of these structures have been hypothesized to result from the action of glaciers or lava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr2005_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr2005-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaeger2007_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaeger2007-73">[71]</a></sup> One of the larger examples, <a href="/wiki/Ma%27adim_Vallis" title="Ma'adim Vallis">Ma'adim Vallis</a> is 700 km (430 mi) long, much greater than the Grand Canyon, with a width of 20 km (12 mi) and a depth of 2 km (1.2 mi) in places. It is thought to have been carved by flowing water early in Mars's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lucchita_rosanova_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-lucchita_rosanova-74">[72]</a></sup> The youngest of these channels are thought to have formed as recently as only a few million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature434_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature434-75">[73]</a></sup> Elsewhere, particularly on the oldest areas of the Martian surface, finer-scale, dendritic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valley_networks_(Mars)" title="Valley networks (Mars)">networks of valleys</a> are spread across significant proportions of the landscape. Features of these valleys and their distribution strongly imply that they were carved by <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a> resulting from precipitation in early Mars history. Subsurface water flow and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_sapping" title="Groundwater sapping">groundwater sapping</a> may play important subsidiary roles in some networks, but precipitation was probably the root cause of the incision in almost all cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CraddockHoward2002_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-CraddockHoward2002-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Along crater and canyon walls, there are thousands of features that appear similar to terrestrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gullies" title="Gullies">gullies</a>. The gullies tend to be in the highlands of the southern hemisphere and to face the Equator; all are poleward of 30° latitude. A number of authors have suggested that their formation process involves liquid water, probably from melting ice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci288_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci288-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa061206_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa061206-78">[76]</a></sup> although others have argued for formation mechanisms involving carbon dioxide frost or the movement of dry dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc061206_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc061206-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa061206b_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa061206b-80">[78]</a></sup> No partially degraded gullies have formed by weathering and no superimposed impact craters have been observed, indicating that these are young features, possibly still active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa061206_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa061206-78">[76]</a></sup> Other geological features, such as <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">deltas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alluvial_fans" title="Alluvial fans">alluvial fans</a> preserved in craters, are further evidence for warmer, wetter conditions at an interval or intervals in earlier Mars history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis2006_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2006-81">[79]</a></sup> Such conditions necessarily require the widespread presence of <a href="/wiki/Crater_lake" title="Crater lake">crater lakes</a> across a large proportion of the surface, for which there is independent mineralogical, sedimentological and geomorphological evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matsubara2011_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matsubara2011-82">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1179" data-file-width="1883" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png/220px-PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png/330px-PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png/440px-PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA16791-MarsCuriosityRover-Composition-YellowknifeBayRocks.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composition of <a href="/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#2012_–_Curiosity_rover_(Mars_Science_Laboratory)" title="List of rocks on Mars">"Yellowknife Bay" rocks</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vein_(geology)" title="Vein (geology)">Rock veins</a> are higher in <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> than "portage" soil (<a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)#Alpha_Particle_X-ray_Spectrometer_(APXS)" title="Curiosity (rover)">APXS</a>, 2013).</div></div></div>
<p>Further evidence that liquid water once existed on the surface of Mars comes from the detection of specific minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goethite" title="Goethite">goethite</a>, both of which sometimes form in the presence of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa040303_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa040303-83">[81]</a></sup> In 2004, <i>Opportunity</i> detected the mineral <a href="/wiki/Jarosite" title="Jarosite">jarosite</a>. This forms only in the presence of acidic water, which demonstrates that water once existed on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa101001_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa101001-84">[82]</a></sup> More recent evidence for liquid water comes from the finding of the mineral <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> on the surface by NASA's Mars rover Opportunity in December 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic-86">[84]</a></sup> It is believed that the amount of water in the upper mantle of Mars, represented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_ion" title="Hydroxyl ion">hydroxyl ions</a> contained within the minerals of Mars's geology, is equal to or greater than that of Earth at 50–300 parts per million of water, which is enough to cover the entire planet to a depth of 200–1,000 m (660–3,280 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic1_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, radar data revealed the presence of large quantities of water ice at the poles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-specials1_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-specials1-68">[66]</a></sup> and at mid-latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsg.utexas.edu_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-jsg.utexas.edu-70">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa050221_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa050221-88">[86]</a></sup> The Mars rover <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_rover" title="Spirit rover">Spirit</a></i> sampled chemical compounds containing water molecules in March 2007. The <i><a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)">Phoenix</a></i> lander directly sampled water ice in shallow Martian soil on July 31, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spacecraft1_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-spacecraft1-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 18, 2013, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> reported evidence from instruments on the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mineral_hydration" title="Mineral hydration">mineral hydration</a>, likely hydrated <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfate" title="Calcium sulfate">calcium sulfate</a>, in several <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock samples</a> including the broken fragments of <a href="/wiki/Tintina_(rock)" title="Tintina (rock)">"Tintina" rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity" title="List of rocks on Mars">"Sutton Inlier" rock</a> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Vein_(geology)" title="Vein (geology)">veins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nodule_(geology)" title="Nodule (geology)">nodules</a> in other rocks like <a href="/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity" title="List of rocks on Mars">"Knorr" rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_rocks_on_Mars#Curiosity" title="List of rocks on Mars">"Wernicke" rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130318_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130318-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20130319_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20130319-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSN-20130120_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSN-20130120-91">[89]</a></sup> Analysis using the rover's <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)#Dynamic_Albedo_of_Neutrons_(DAN)" title="Curiosity (rover)">DAN instrument</a> provided evidence of subsurface water, amounting to as much as 4% water content, down to a depth of 60 cm (24 in), during the rover's traverse from the <i><a href="/wiki/Bradbury_Landing" title="Bradbury Landing">Bradbury Landing</a></i> site to the <i>Yellowknife Bay</i> area in the <i><a href="/wiki/Glenelg,_Mars" title="Glenelg, Mars">Glenelg</a></i> terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130318_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130318-89">[87]</a></sup> In September 2015, NASA announced that they had found conclusive evidence of hydrated <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> flows on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recurring_slope_lineae" title="Recurring slope lineae">recurring slope lineae</a>, based on spectrometer readings of the darkened areas of slopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ojhaetal2015_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ojhaetal2015-94">[92]</a></sup> These observations provided confirmation of earlier hypotheses based on timing of formation and their rate of growth, that these dark streaks resulted from water flowing in the very shallow subsurface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeasonalFlows_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-SeasonalFlows-95">[93]</a></sup> The streaks contain hydrated salts, perchlorates, which have water molecules in their crystal structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> The streaks flow downhill in Martian summer, when the temperature is above −23 degrees Celsius, and freeze at lower temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> On September 28, 2015, NASA <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_discoveries_of_water_on_Mars" title="Chronology of discoveries of water on Mars">announced the presence of</a> briny flowing <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">salt water</a> on the Martian surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers believe that much of the low northern plains of the planet were <a href="/wiki/Mars_ocean_hypothesis" title="Mars ocean hypothesis">covered with an ocean</a> hundreds of meters deep, though this remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Head1999_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Head1999-99">[97]</a></sup> In March 2015, scientists stated that such an ocean might have been the size of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>. This finding was derived from the ratio of water to <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> in the modern Martian atmosphere compared to that ratio on Earth. The amount of Martian deuterium is eight times the amount that exists on Earth, suggesting that ancient Mars had significantly higher levels of water. Results from the <i>Curiosity</i> rover had previously found a high ratio of deuterium in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gale_Crater" title="Gale Crater">Gale Crater</a>, though not significantly high enough to suggest the former presence of an ocean. Other scientists caution that these results have not been confirmed, and point out that Martian climate models have not yet shown that the planet was warm enough in the past to support bodies of liquid water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150305_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150305-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_caps">Polar caps</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps" title="Martian polar ice caps">Martian polar ice caps</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:167px;max-width:167px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:159px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1319" data-file-width="1368" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg/165px-Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg/248px-Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg/330px-Martian_north_polar_cap.jpg 2x" width="165"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">North polar early summer ice cap (1999)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:221px;max-width:221px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:159px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1234" data-file-width="1690" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg/219px-South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg/329px-South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg/438px-South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg 2x" width="219"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">South polar midsummer ice cap (2000)</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>Mars has two permanent polar ice caps. During a pole's winter, it lies in continuous darkness, chilling the surface and causing the <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(phase_transition)" title="Deposition (phase transition)">deposition</a> of 25–30% of the atmosphere into slabs of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> ice (<a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus169_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus169-101">[99]</a></sup> When the poles are again exposed to sunlight, the frozen CO<sub>2</sub> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(physics)" title="Sublimation (physics)">sublimes</a>. These seasonal actions transport large amounts of dust and water vapor, giving rise to Earth-like frost and large <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_cloud" title="Cirrus cloud">cirrus clouds</a>. Clouds of water-ice were photographed by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opportunity_rover" title="Opportunity rover">Opportunity</a></i> rover in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clouds_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-clouds-102">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>The caps at both poles consist primarily (70%) of water ice. Frozen carbon dioxide accumulates as a comparatively thin layer about one metre thick on the north cap in the northern winter only, whereas the south cap has a permanent dry ice cover about eight metres thick. This permanent dry ice cover at the south pole is peppered by <a href="/wiki/Swiss_cheese_features" title="Swiss cheese features">flat floored, shallow, roughly circular pits</a>, which repeat imaging shows are expanding by meters per year; this suggests that the permanent CO<sub>2</sub> cover over the south pole water ice is degrading over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malin2001_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-malin2001-103">[101]</a></sup> The northern polar cap has a diameter of about 1,000 km (620 mi) during the northern Mars summer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mira_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-mira-104">[102]</a></sup> and contains about 1.6 million cubic kilometres (380,000 cu mi) of ice, which, if spread evenly on the cap, would be 2 km (1.2 mi) thick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown-105">[103]</a></sup> (This compares to a volume of 2.85 million cubic kilometres (680,000 cu mi) for the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a>.) The southern polar cap has a diameter of 350 km (220 mi) and a thickness of 3 km (1.9 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phillips_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-phillips-106">[104]</a></sup> The total volume of ice in the south polar cap plus the adjacent layered deposits has been estimated at 1.6 million cubic km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci315_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci315-107">[105]</a></sup> Both polar caps show spiral troughs, which recent analysis of <a href="/wiki/SHARAD" title="SHARAD">SHARAD</a> ice penetrating radar has shown are a result of <a href="/wiki/Katabatic_wind" title="Katabatic wind">katabatic winds</a> that spiral due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_Effect" title="Coriolis Effect">Coriolis Effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Onset_and_migration_of_spiral_troughs_on_Mars_revealed_by_orbital_radar_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Onset_and_migration_of_spiral_troughs_on_Mars_revealed_by_orbital_radar-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mystery_Spirals_on_Mars_Finally_Explained_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mystery_Spirals_on_Mars_Finally_Explained-109">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The seasonal frosting of areas near the southern ice cap results in the formation of transparent 1-metre-thick slabs of dry ice above the ground. With the arrival of spring, sunlight warms the subsurface and pressure from subliming CO<sub>2</sub> builds up under a slab, elevating and ultimately rupturing it. This leads to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martian_geyser" title="Martian geyser">geyser-like eruptions</a> of CO<sub>2</sub> gas mixed with dark basaltic sand or dust. This process is rapid, observed happening in the space of a few days, weeks or months, a rate of change rather unusual in geology – especially for Mars. The gas rushing underneath a slab to the site of a geyser carves a spiderweb-like pattern of radial channels under the ice, the process being the inverted equivalent of an erosion network formed by water draining through a single plughole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2006-100_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-2006-100-110">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kieffer2000_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kieffer2000-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Portyankina_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Portyankina-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hugh2006_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh2006-113">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_naming_of_surface_features">Geography and naming of surface features</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_topography_(MOLA_dataset)_with_poles_HiRes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6940" data-file-width="15400" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg/220px-Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg/330px-Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg/440px-Mars_topography_%28MOLA_dataset%29_with_poles_HiRes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_topography_(MOLA_dataset)_with_poles_HiRes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Laser_Altimeter" title="Mars Orbiter Laser Altimeter">MOLA</a>-based topographic map showing highlands (red and orange) dominating the southern hemisphere of Mars, lowlands (blue) the northern. Volcanic plateaus delimit regions of the northern plains, whereas the highlands are punctuated by several large impact basins.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1824" data-file-width="2736" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg/220px-PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg/330px-PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg/440px-PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA11176_-_A_Recent_Cluster_of_Impacts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>These new impact craters on Mars occurred sometime between 2008 and 2014, as detected from orbit</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mars" title="Geography of Mars">Geography of Mars</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more on how geographic references are determined, see <a href="/wiki/Geodetic_datum" title="Geodetic datum">Geodetic datum</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Surface_features_of_Mars" title="Category:Surface features of Mars">Category:Surface features of Mars</a></div>
<p>Although better remembered for mapping the Moon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_M%C3%A4dler" title="Johann Heinrich Mädler">Johann Heinrich Mädler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Beer" title="Wilhelm Beer">Wilhelm Beer</a> were the first "areographers". They began by establishing that most of Mars's surface features were permanent and by more precisely determining the planet's rotation period. In 1840, Mädler combined ten years of observations and drew the first map of Mars. Rather than giving names to the various markings, Beer and Mädler simply designated them with letters; Meridian Bay (Sinus Meridiani) was thus feature "<i>a</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheehan_ch04_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-sheehan_ch04-114">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, features on Mars are named from a variety of sources. <a href="/wiki/Classical_albedo_features_on_Mars" title="Classical albedo features on Mars">Albedo features</a> are named for classical mythology. Craters larger than 60 km are named for deceased scientists and writers and others who have contributed to the study of Mars. Craters smaller than 60 km are named for towns and villages of the world with populations of less than 100,000. Large valleys are named for the word "Mars" or "star" in various languages; small valleys are named for rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-115">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Large <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> features retain many of the older names, but are often updated to reflect new knowledge of the nature of the features. For example, <i>Nix Olympica</i> (the snows of Olympus) has become <i><a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a></i> (Mount Olympus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-viking_1950_2000_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-viking_1950_2000-116">[114]</a></sup> The surface of Mars as seen from Earth is divided into two kinds of areas, with differing albedo. The paler plains covered with dust and sand rich in reddish iron oxides were once thought of as Martian "continents" and given names like <a href="/wiki/Arabia_Terra" title="Arabia Terra">Arabia Terra</a> (<i>land of Arabia</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Amazonis_Planitia" title="Amazonis Planitia">Amazonis Planitia</a> (<i>Amazonian plain</i>). The dark features were thought to be seas, hence their names <a href="/wiki/Mare_Erythraeum" title="Mare Erythraeum">Mare Erythraeum</a>, Mare Sirenum and <a href="/wiki/Aurorae_Sinus" title="Aurorae Sinus">Aurorae Sinus</a>. The largest dark feature seen from Earth is <a href="/wiki/Syrtis_Major_Planum" title="Syrtis Major Planum">Syrtis Major Planum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seds_huygens_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-seds_huygens-117">[115]</a></sup> The permanent northern polar ice cap is named <a href="/wiki/Planum_Boreum" title="Planum Boreum">Planum Boreum</a>, whereas the southern cap is called <a href="/wiki/Planum_Australe" title="Planum Australe">Planum Australe</a>.
</p><p>Mars's equator is defined by its rotation, but the location of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">Prime Meridian</a> was specified, as was Earth's (at <a href="/wiki/Greenwich" title="Greenwich">Greenwich</a>), by choice of an arbitrary point; Mädler and Beer selected a line for their first maps of Mars in 1830. After the spacecraft <a href="/wiki/Mariner_9" title="Mariner 9">Mariner 9</a> provided extensive imagery of Mars in 1972, a small crater (later called <a href="/wiki/Airy-0" title="Airy-0">Airy-0</a>), located in the <a href="/wiki/Sinus_Meridiani" title="Sinus Meridiani">Sinus Meridiani</a> ("Middle Bay" or "Meridian Bay"), was chosen for the definition of 0.0° longitude to coincide with the original selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archinal_caplinger_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-archinal_caplinger-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because Mars has no oceans and hence no "<a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>", a zero-elevation surface had to be selected as a reference level; this is called the <i>areoid</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAMola2007_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAMola2007-119">[117]</a></sup> of Mars, analogous to the terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Geoid" title="Geoid">geoid</a>. Zero altitude was defined by the height at which there is 610.5 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a> (6.105 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a>) of atmospheric pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pers66_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-pers66-120">[118]</a></sup> This pressure corresponds to the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of water, and it is about 0.6% of the sea level surface pressure on Earth (0.006 atm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lunine99_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-lunine99-121">[119]</a></sup> In practice, today this surface is defined directly from satellite gravity measurements.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Map_of_quadrangles">Map of quadrangles</span></h4>
<p>For mapping purposes, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> divides the surface of Mars into thirty "<a href="/wiki/Quadrangle_(geography)" title="Quadrangle (geography)">quadrangles</a>", each named for a prominent physiographic feature within that quadrangle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mapping_mars_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-mapping_mars-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup> The quadrangles can be seen and explored via the interactive image map below.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

North is at the top; <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Mars&amp;params=0_N_180_W_globe:Mars"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">0°N</span> <span class="longitude">180°W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">0°N 180°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">0; -180</span></span></span></a></span> is at the far left on the <a href="/wiki/Mars#Geography" title="Mars">equator</a>.  The map images were taken by the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a>. <div style="float: right;">[<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Mars_Quads_-_By_Name" title="Template:Mars Quads - By Name"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Mars_Quads_-_By_Name" title="Template talk:Mars Quads - By Name"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li></ul></div>]</div></div>
