<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_topography">Impact topography</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1393" data-file-width="2080" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg/220px-PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg/330px-PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg/440px-PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA15038_Spirit_Lander_and_Bonneville_Crater_in_Color.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonneville crater and <i>Spirit</i> rover's lander</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Martian_dichotomy" title="Martian dichotomy">dichotomy</a> of Martian topography is striking: northern plains flattened by lava flows contrast with the southern highlands, pitted and cratered by ancient impacts. Research in 2008 has presented evidence regarding a theory proposed in 1980 postulating that, four billion years ago, the northern hemisphere of Mars was struck by an object one-tenth to two-thirds the size of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. If validated, this would make the northern hemisphere of Mars the site of an <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact crater</a> 10,600 by 8,500 km (6,600 by 5,300 mi) in size, or roughly the area of Europe, Asia, and Australia combined, surpassing the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole%E2%80%93Aitken_basin" title="South Pole–Aitken basin">South Pole–Aitken basin</a> as the largest impact crater in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-northcratersn_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-northcratersn-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-northcraterguard_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-northcraterguard-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="928" height="57" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg/220px-PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg/330px-PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg/440px-PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18381-Mars-FreshAsteroidImpact2012-Before27March-After28March.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fresh <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> impact on Mars at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Mars&amp;params=3.34_N_219.38_E_globe:Mars"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">3°20′N</span> <span class="longitude">219°23′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">3.34°N 219.38°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">3.34; 219.38</span></span></span></a></span>. These before and after images of the same site were taken on the Martian afternoons of March 27 and 28, 2012 respectively (<a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">MRO</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140522_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140522-126">[124]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Mars is scarred by a number of impact craters: a total of 43,000 craters with a diameter of 5 km (3.1 mi) or greater have been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wright03_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-wright03-127">[125]</a></sup> The largest confirmed of these is the <a href="/wiki/Hellas_Planitia" title="Hellas Planitia">Hellas impact basin</a>, a light <a href="/wiki/Albedo_feature" title="Albedo feature">albedo feature</a> clearly visible from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucar_geography_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucar_geography-128">[126]</a></sup> Due to the smaller mass of Mars, the probability of an object colliding with the planet is about half that of Earth. Mars is located closer to the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, so it has an increased chance of being struck by materials from that source. Mars is more likely to be struck by short-period <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, <i>i.e.</i>, those that lie within the orbit of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emp9_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-emp9-129">[127]</a></sup> In spite of this, there are far fewer craters on Mars compared with the Moon, because the atmosphere of Mars provides protection against small meteors and surface modifying processes have erased some craters.
</p><p>Martian craters can have a morphology that suggests the ground became wet after the meteor impacted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emp45_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-emp45-130">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanoes">Volcanoes</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1883" data-file-width="2020" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg/220px-Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg/330px-Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg/440px-Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Olympus_Mons_alt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Viking_1" title="Viking 1">Viking 1</a> image of <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a>. The volcano and related terrain are approximately 550 km (340 mi) across.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Volcanology_of_Mars" title="Volcanology of Mars">Volcanology of Mars</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shield_volcano" title="Shield volcano">shield volcano</a> <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a> (<i>Mount Olympus</i>) is an extinct volcano in the vast upland region <a href="/wiki/Tharsis" title="Tharsis">Tharsis</a>, which contains several other large volcanoes. Olympus Mons is roughly three times the height of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a>, which in comparison stands at just over 8.8 km (5.5 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scsdes49_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-scsdes49-131">[129]</a></sup> It is either the tallest or second-tallest mountain in the Solar System, depending on how it is measured, with various sources giving figures ranging from about 21 to 27 km (13 to 17 mi) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glenday09_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-glenday09-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tectonic_sites">Tectonic sites</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2600" data-file-width="6000" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg/220px-016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg/330px-016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg/440px-016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:016vallesmarineris_reduced0.25.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">Valles Marineris</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">2001 Mars Odyssey</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>The large canyon, <a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">Valles Marineris</a> (Latin for "<a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner</a> Valleys", also known as Agathadaemon in the old canal maps), has a length of 4,000 km (2,500 mi) and a depth of up to 7 km (4.3 mi). The length of Valles Marineris is equivalent to the length of Europe and extends across one-fifth the circumference of Mars. By comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a> on Earth is only 446 km (277 mi) long and nearly 2 km (1.2 mi) deep. Valles Marineris was formed due to the swelling of the <a href="/wiki/Tharsis" title="Tharsis">Tharsis</a> area, which caused the crust in the area of Valles Marineris to collapse. In 2012, it was proposed that Valles Marineris is not just a <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">graben</a>, but a plate boundary where 150 km (93 mi) of <a href="/wiki/Transform_fault" title="Transform fault">transverse motion</a> has occurred, making Mars a planet with possibly a two-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plate</a> arrangement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tectonic_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-tectonic-134">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lin,_An_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lin,_An-135">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Holes">Holes</span></h4>
<p>Images from the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_Emission_Imaging_System" title="Thermal Emission Imaging System">Thermal Emission Imaging System</a> (THEMIS) aboard NASA's <a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">Mars Odyssey orbiter</a> have revealed seven possible <a href="/wiki/Cave" title="Cave">cave</a> entrances on the flanks of the volcano <a href="/wiki/Arsia_Mons" title="Arsia Mons">Arsia Mons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cushing_titus_wynn07_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-cushing_titus_wynn07-136">[134]</a></sup> The caves, named after loved ones of their discoverers, are collectively known as the "seven sisters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nau070328_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-nau070328-137">[135]</a></sup> Cave entrances measure from 100 to 252 m (328 to 827 ft) wide and they are estimated to be at least 73 to 96 m (240 to 315 ft) deep. Because light does not reach the floor of most of the caves, it is possible that they extend much deeper than these lower estimates and widen below the surface. "Dena" is the only exception; its floor is visible and was measured to be 130 m (430 ft) deep. The interiors of these caverns may be protected from micrometeoroids, UV radiation, <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a> and high energy particles that bombard the planet's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc070317_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc070317-138">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Atmosphere of Mars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_atmosphere.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1349" data-file-width="1205" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mars_atmosphere.jpg/170px-Mars_atmosphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mars_atmosphere.jpg/255px-Mars_atmosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mars_atmosphere.jpg/340px-Mars_atmosphere.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_atmosphere.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tenuous <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere of Mars</a> visible on the horizon</div></div></div>
<p>Mars lost its <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> 4 billion years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swind_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-swind-139">[137]</a></sup> possibly because of numerous asteroid strikes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> so the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> interacts directly with the Martian <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>, lowering the atmospheric density by stripping away atoms from the outer layer. Both <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a> have detected ionised atmospheric particles trailing off into space behind Mars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swind_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-swind-139">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swind2_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-swind2-141">[139]</a></sup> and this atmospheric loss is being studied by the <a href="/wiki/MAVEN" title="MAVEN">MAVEN</a> orbiter. Compared to Earth, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmosphere</a> of Mars is quite rarefied. <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">Atmospheric pressure</a> on the surface today ranges from a low of 30 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a> (0.030 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a>) on <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a> to over 1,155 Pa (1.155 kPa) in <a href="/wiki/Hellas_Planitia" title="Hellas Planitia">Hellas Planitia</a>, with a mean pressure at the surface level of 600 Pa (0.60 kPa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bolonkin09_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-bolonkin09-142">[140]</a></sup> The highest atmospheric density on Mars is equal to that found 35 km (22 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atkinson07_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-atkinson07-143">[141]</a></sup> above Earth's surface. The resulting mean surface pressure is only 0.6% of that of Earth (101.3 kPa). The <a href="/wiki/Scale_height" title="Scale height">scale height</a> of the atmosphere is about 10.8 km (6.7 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carr06_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-carr06-144">[142]</a></sup> which is higher than Earth's, 6 km (3.7 mi), because the surface <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Mars" title="Gravity of Mars">gravity of Mars</a> is only about 38% of Earth's, an effect offset by both the lower temperature and 50% higher average molecular weight of the atmosphere of Mars.
</p><p>The atmosphere of Mars consists of about 96% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, 1.93% <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> and 1.89% <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> along with traces of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abundance_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abundance-145">[143]</a></sup> The atmosphere is quite dusty, containing particulates about 1.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" title="Μm">µm</a> in diameter which give the Martian sky a <a href="/wiki/Tawny_(color)" title="Tawny (color)">tawny</a> color when seen from the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dusty_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-dusty-146">[144]</a></sup> It may take on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pink_(color)" title="Pink (color)">pink</a> hue due to <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> particles suspended in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rees2012_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rees2012-17">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1440" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/220px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/330px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png/440px-PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19088-MarsCuriosityRover-MethaneSource-20141216.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Potential sources and sinks of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane" title="Atmosphere of Mars">methane</a> (CH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>) on Mars</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> has been detected in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars#Methane" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Martian atmosphere</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methane-me_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-methane-me-147">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-methane_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-methane-148">[146]</a></sup> it occurs in extended plumes, and the profiles imply that the methane is released from discrete regions. The concentration of methane fluctuates from about 0.24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a> during the northern winter to about 0.65 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a> during the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sample2018_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sample2018-149">[147]</a></sup> In northern midsummer 2003, the principal plume contained 19,000 metric tons of methane, with an estimated source strength of 0.6 kilograms per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plumes_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-plumes-150">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hand08_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-hand08-151">[149]</a></sup> The profiles suggest that there may be two local source regions, the first centered near <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Mars&amp;params=30_N_260_W_globe:Mars"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">30°N</span> <span class="longitude">260°W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">30°N 260°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">30; -260</span></span></span></a></span> and the second near <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Mars&amp;params=0_N_310_W_globe:Mars"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">0°N</span> <span class="longitude">310°W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">0°N 310°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">0; -310</span></span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plumes_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-plumes-150">[148]</a></sup> It is estimated that Mars must produce 270 tonnes per year of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plumes_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-plumes-150">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-results_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-results-152">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane can exist in the Martian atmosphere for only a limited period before it is destroyed—estimates of its lifetime range from 0.6–4 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plumes_150-3"><a href="#cite_note-plumes-150">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature460_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature460-153">[151]</a></sup> Its presence despite this short lifetime indicates that an active source of the gas must be present. <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">Volcanic</a> activity, <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">cometary</a> impacts, and the presence of <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbial</a> life forms are among possible sources. Methane could be produced by a non-biological process called <i><a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">serpentinization</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serpentinization_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-serpentinization-154">[c]</a></sup> involving water, carbon dioxide, and the mineral <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, which is known to be common on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olivine_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-olivine-155">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2045" data-file-width="5170" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg/300px-PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg/450px-PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg/600px-PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18613-MarsMAVEN-Atmosphere-3UV-Views-20141014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Escaping atmosphere</a> on Mars (<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>) by <a href="/wiki/MAVEN" title="MAVEN">MAVEN</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet%E2%80%93visible_spectroscopy" title="Ultraviolet–visible spectroscopy">UV</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141014-NJ_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141014-NJ-156">[153]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i></a> rover, which landed on Mars in August 2012, is able to make measurements that distinguish between different isotopologues of methane,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> but even if the mission is to determine that microscopic Martian life is the source of the methane, the life forms likely reside far below the surface, outside of the rover's reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup> The first measurements with the <a href="/wiki/Sample_Analysis_at_Mars" title="Sample Analysis at Mars">Tunable Laser Spectrometer (TLS)</a> indicated that there is less than 5 ppb of methane at the landing site at the point of the measurement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science-20121102_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science-20121102-160">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20121102_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20121102-161">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20121102_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20121102-162">[159]</a></sup> On September 19, 2013, NASA scientists, from further measurements by <i>Curiosity</i>, reported no detection of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">atmospheric methane</a> with a measured value of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999180000000000000♠</span>0.18<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.67</span> ppbv corresponding to an upper limit of only 1.3 ppbv (95% confidence limit) and, as a result, conclude that the probability of current methanogenic microbial activity on Mars is reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SJ-20130919_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-SJ-20130919-163">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130919_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130919-164">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130919_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130919-165">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission" title="Mars Orbiter Mission">Mars Orbiter Mission</a> by <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is searching for methane in the atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-payload_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-payload-166">[163]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/ExoMars_Trace_Gas_Orbiter" title="ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter">ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter</a>, launched in 2016, would further study the methane as well as its decomposition products, such as <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mustard_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mustard-167">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 16, 2014, NASA reported the <i>Curiosity</i> rover detected a "tenfold spike", likely localized, in the amount of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Martian atmosphere</a>. Sample measurements taken "a dozen times over 20 months" showed increases in late 2013 and early 2014, averaging "7 parts of methane per billion in the atmosphere." Before and after that, readings averaged around one-tenth that level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141216-GW_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141216-GW-168">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141216-KC_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141216-KC-169">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ammonia was tentatively detected on Mars by the Mars Express satellite, but with its relatively short lifetime, it is not clear what produced it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidw_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-davidw-170">[167]</a></sup> Ammonia is not stable in the Martian atmosphere and breaks down after a few hours. One possible source is volcanic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidw_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-davidw-170">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2017, NASA reported <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> levels on the surface of the planet Mars were temporarily <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(radiation)" title="Orders of magnitude (radiation)">doubled</a>, and were associated with an <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurora</a> 25 times brighter than any observed earlier, due to a massive, and unexpected, <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">solar storm</a> in the middle of the month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20170929_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20170929-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aurora">Aurora</span></h4>
<p>In 1994, the European Space Agency's <a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a> found an ultraviolet glow coming from "magnetic umbrellas" in the southern hemisphere. Mars does not have a global magnetic field which guides charged particles entering the atmosphere. Mars has multiple umbrella-shaped magnetic fields mainly in the southern hemisphere, which are remnants of a global field that decayed billions of years ago.
</p><p>In late December 2014, NASA's MAVEN spacecraft detected evidence of widespread auroras in Mars's northern hemisphere and descended to approximately 20–30 degrees North latitude of Mars's equator. The particles causing the aurora penetrated into the Martian atmosphere, creating auroras below 100 km above the surface, Earth's auroras range from 100 km to 500 km above the surface. Magnetic fields in the solar wind drape over Mars, into the atmosphere, and the charged particles follow the solar wind magnetic field lines into the atmosphere, causing auroras to occur outside the magnetic umbrellas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 18, 2015, NASA reported the detection of an <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurora</a> that is not fully understood and an unexplained dust cloud in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere of Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150318_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150318-173">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Mars" title="Climate of Mars">Climate of Mars</a></div>
<p>Of all the planets in the Solar System, the seasons of Mars are the most Earth-like, due to the similar tilts of the two planets' rotational axes. The lengths of the Martian seasons are about twice those of Earth's because Mars's greater distance from the Sun leads to the Martian year being about two Earth years long. Martian surface temperatures vary from lows of about −143 °C (−225 °F) at the winter polar caps<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cold_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-cold-13">[11]</a></sup> to highs of up to 35 °C (95 °F) in equatorial summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hot_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-hot-14">[12]</a></sup> The wide range in temperatures is due to the thin atmosphere which cannot store much solar heat, the low atmospheric pressure, and the low <a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity" title="Volumetric heat capacity">thermal inertia</a> of Martian soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_surface_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_surface-174">[171]</a></sup> The planet is 1.52 times as far from the Sun as Earth, resulting in just 43% of the amount of sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disc920901_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-disc920901-175">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>If Mars had an Earth-like orbit, its seasons would be similar to Earth's because its <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> is similar to Earth's. The comparatively large <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> of the Martian orbit has a significant effect. Mars is near <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a> when it is summer in the southern hemisphere and winter in the north, and near <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">aphelion</a> when it is winter in the southern hemisphere and summer in the north. As a result, the seasons in the southern hemisphere are more extreme and the seasons in the northern are milder than would otherwise be the case. The summer temperatures in the south can be warmer than the equivalent summer temperatures in the north by up to 30 °C (54 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodman97_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-goodman97-176">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mars has the largest <a href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">dust storms</a> in the Solar System, reaching speeds of over 160 km/h (100 mph). These can vary from a storm over a small area, to gigantic storms that cover the entire planet. They tend to occur when Mars is closest to the Sun, and have been shown to increase the global temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philips01_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-philips01-177">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA22487-Mars-BeforeAfterDust-20180719.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="1000" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA22487-Mars-BeforeAfterDust-20180719.gif/500px-PIA22487-Mars-BeforeAfterDust-20180719.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA22487-Mars-BeforeAfterDust-20180719.gif/750px-PIA22487-Mars-BeforeAfterDust-20180719.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/PIA22487-Mars-BeforeAfterDust-20180719.gif 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA22487-Mars-BeforeAfterDust-20180719.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Mars (before/after) dust storm (July 2018)</center></div></div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbit_of_Mars" title="Orbit of Mars">Orbit of Mars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marsorbitsolarsystem.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="250" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Marsorbitsolarsystem.gif/220px-Marsorbitsolarsystem.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Marsorbitsolarsystem.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marsorbitsolarsystem.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mars is about 230 million km (143 million mi) from the Sun; its orbital period is 687 (Earth) days, depicted in red. Earth's orbit is in blue.</div></div></div>
<p>Mars's average distance from the Sun is roughly 230 million km (143 million mi), and its orbital period is 687 (Earth) days. The solar day (or <a href="/wiki/Timekeeping_on_Mars" title="Timekeeping on Mars">sol</a>) on Mars is only slightly longer than an Earth day: 24 hours, 39 minutes, and 35.244 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup> A Martian year is equal to 1.8809 Earth years, or 1 year, 320 days, and 18.2 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The axial tilt of Mars is 25.19 degrees relative to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_plane" title="Orbital plane">orbital plane</a>, which is similar to the axial tilt of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-10">[8]</a></sup> As a result, Mars has seasons like Earth, though on Mars they are nearly twice as long because its orbital period is that much longer. In the present day epoch, the orientation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_pole" title="North pole">north pole</a> of Mars is close to the star <a href="/wiki/Deneb" title="Deneb">Deneb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barlow08_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-barlow08-15">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mars has a relatively pronounced <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> of about 0.09; of the seven other planets in the Solar System, only <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> has a larger orbital eccentricity. It is known that in the past, Mars has had a much more circular orbit. At one point, 1.35 million Earth years ago, Mars had an eccentricity of roughly 0.002, much less than that of Earth today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars_eccentricity_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-mars_eccentricity-180">[177]</a></sup> Mars's cycle of eccentricity is 96,000 Earth years compared to Earth's cycle of 100,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meeus2003_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meeus2003-181">[178]</a></sup> Mars has a much longer cycle of eccentricity, with a period of 2.2 million Earth years, and this overshadows the 96,000-year cycle in the eccentricity graphs. For the last 35,000 years, the orbit of Mars has been getting slightly more eccentric because of the gravitational effects of the other planets. The closest distance between Earth and Mars will continue to mildly decrease for the next 25,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baalke2003_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baalke2003-182">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitability_and_search_for_life">Habitability and search for life</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">Life on Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viking_lander_biological_experiments" title="Viking lander biological experiments">Viking lander biological experiments</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Mars" title="Colonization of Mars">Colonization of Mars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_Viking_11d128.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="1042" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Mars_Viking_11d128.png/220px-Mars_Viking_11d128.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Mars_Viking_11d128.png/330px-Mars_Viking_11d128.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Mars_Viking_11d128.png/440px-Mars_Viking_11d128.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_Viking_11d128.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Viking 1 lander's sampling arm scooped up soil samples for tests (<a href="/wiki/Chryse_Planitia" title="Chryse Planitia">Chryse Planitia</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The current understanding of <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">planetary habitability</a>—the ability of a world to develop environmental conditions favorable to the emergence of life—favors planets that have liquid water on their surface. Most often this requires the orbit of a planet to lie within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_Habitability_Index" title="Planetary Habitability Index">habitable zone</a>, which for the Sun extends from just beyond Venus to about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nowack_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nowack-183">[180]</a></sup> During perihelion, Mars dips inside this region, but Mars's thin (low-pressure) atmosphere prevents liquid water from existing over large regions for extended periods. The past flow of liquid water demonstrates the planet's potential for habitability. Recent evidence has suggested that any water on the Martian surface may have been too salty and acidic to support regular terrestrial life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saltlife_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-saltlife-184">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="938" data-file-width="1551" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg/220px-PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg/330px-PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg/440px-PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19673-Mars-AlgaCrater-ImpactGlassDetected-MRO-20150608.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detection of <a href="/wiki/Impactite" title="Impactite">impact glass</a> deposits (green spots) at <a href="/wiki/List_of_craters_on_Mars:_A%E2%80%93G" title="List of craters on Mars: A–G">Alga crater</a>, a possible site for preserved <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">ancient life</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150608_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150608-185">[182]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The lack of a magnetosphere and the extremely thin atmosphere of Mars are a challenge: the planet has little <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">heat transfer</a> across its surface, poor insulation against bombardment of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> and insufficient atmospheric pressure to retain water in a liquid form (water instead <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimes</a> to a gaseous state). Mars is nearly, or perhaps totally, geologically dead; the end of volcanic activity has apparently stopped the recycling of chemicals and minerals between the surface and interior of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hannsson97_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-hannsson97-186">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>In situ</i> investigations have been performed on Mars by the <a href="/wiki/Viking_program#Viking_landers" title="Viking program"><i>Viking</i> landers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)"><i>Spirit</i></a> and <i>Opportunity</i> rovers, <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)"><i>Phoenix</i></a> lander, and <i>Curiosity</i> rover. Evidence suggests that the planet was once significantly more habitable than it is today, but whether living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> ever existed there remains unknown. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking_probes" title="Viking probes">Viking probes</a> of the mid-1970s carried experiments designed to detect microorganisms in Martian soil at their respective landing sites and had positive results, including a temporary increase of CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span> production on exposure to water and nutrients. This sign of life was later disputed by scientists, resulting in a continuing debate, with NASA scientist <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Levin" title="Gilbert Levin">Gilbert Levin</a> asserting that Viking may have found life. A re-analysis of the Viking data, in light of modern knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophile</a> forms of life, has suggested that the Viking tests were not sophisticated enough to detect these forms of life. The tests could even have killed a (hypothetical) life form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSU2006_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSU2006-187">[184]</a></sup> Tests conducted by the Phoenix Mars lander have shown that the soil has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline" title="Alkaline">alkaline</a> <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> and it contains magnesium, sodium, potassium and chloride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nutrient_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-nutrient-188">[185]</a></sup> The soil nutrients may be able to support life, but life would still have to be shielded from the intense ultraviolet light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UV_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-UV-189">[186]</a></sup> A recent analysis of martian meteorite EETA79001 found 0.6 ppm ClO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;">−<br/>4</span>, 1.4 ppm ClO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;">−<br/>3</span>, and 16 ppm NO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;">−<br/>3</span>, most likely of Martian origin. The ClO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;">−<br/>3</span> suggests the presence of other highly oxidizing oxychlorines, such as ClO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;">−<br/>2</span> or ClO, produced both by UV oxidation of Cl and X-ray radiolysis of ClO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;">−<br/>4</span>. Thus, only highly refractory and/or well-protected (sub-surface) organics or life forms are likely to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1184" data-file-width="1584" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg/220px-Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg/330px-Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg/440px-Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars-Curiosity-RockStructures-20180102.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This image from Gale crater in 2018 prompted speculation that some shapes were worm-like fossils, but they were geological formations probably formed under water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa2018-02_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa2018-02-191">[188]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A 2014 analysis of the Phoenix WCL showed that the Ca(ClO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>)<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span> in the Phoenix soil has not interacted with liquid water of any form, perhaps for as long as 600 Myr. If it had, the highly soluble Ca(ClO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>)<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span> in contact with liquid water would have formed only CaSO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>. This suggests a severely arid environment, with minimal or no liquid water interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists have proposed that carbonate globules found in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a> <a href="/wiki/Allan_Hills_84001" title="Allan Hills 84001">ALH84001</a>, which is thought to have originated from Mars, could be fossilized microbes extant on Mars when the meteorite was blasted from the Martian surface by a meteor strike some 15 million years ago. This proposal has been met with skepticism, and an exclusively inorganic origin for the shapes has been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-am89_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-am89-193">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Small quantities of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> detected by Mars orbiters are both claimed to be possible evidence for life, as these <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> would quickly break down in the Martian atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus172_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus172-194">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-form_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-form-195">[192]</a></sup> Alternatively, these compounds may instead be replenished by volcanic or other geological means, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpentinization" title="Serpentinization">serpentinization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olivine_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-olivine-155">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1448" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg/170px-Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg/255px-Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg/340px-Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars-SubglacialWater-SouthPoleRegion-20180725.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Location of subsurface water in <a href="/wiki/Planum_Australe" title="Planum Australe">Planum Australe</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Impactite" title="Impactite">Impact glass</a>, formed by the impact of meteors, which on Earth can preserve signs of life, has been found on the surface of the impact craters on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown20140418_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown20140418-196">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz2014_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schultz2014-197">[194]</a></sup> Likewise, the glass in impact craters on Mars could have preserved signs of life if life existed at the site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasapr20150608_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasapr20150608-198">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown20150608_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown20150608-199">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam20150612_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam20150612-200">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2017, evidence of the <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">earliest known life</a> <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life#Colonization_of_land" title="Evolutionary history of life">on land</a> on Earth may have been found in 3.48-billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Geyserite" title="Geyserite">geyserite</a> and other related mineral deposits (often found around <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>) uncovered in the <a href="/wiki/Pilbara_Craton" title="Pilbara Craton">Pilbara Craton</a> of <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>. These findings may be helpful in deciding where best to search for early signs of <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">life on the planet Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PO-20170509_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-PO-20170509-201">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC-20170509_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC-20170509-202">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 2018, media reports speculated that certain rock features at a site called Jura looked like a type of fossil, but project scientists say the formations likely resulted from a geological process at the bottom of an ancient drying lakebed, and are related to mineral veins in the area similar to <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa2018-02_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa2018-02-191">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 7, 2018, NASA announced that the <i>Curiosity</i> rover had discovered <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> in sedimentary rocks dating to three billion years old,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[200]</a></sup> indicating that some of the building blocks for life were present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC-20180607_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPC-20180607-204">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180607_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180607-205">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2018, scientists reported the discovery of a subglacial lake on Mars, the first known stable body of water on the planet. It sits 1.5 km (0.9 mi) below the surface at the base of the <a href="/wiki/Planum_Australe" title="Planum Australe">southern polar ice cap</a> and is about 20 km (12 mi) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180725_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180725-206">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180725_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180725-207">[204]</a></sup> The lake was discovered using the <a href="/wiki/MARSIS" title="MARSIS">MARSIS</a> radar on board the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a></i> orbiter, and the profiles were collected between May 2012 and December 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suppl_material_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suppl_material-208">[205]</a></sup> The lake is centered at 193°E, 81°S, a flat area that does not exhibit any peculiar topographic characteristics. It is mostly surrounded by higher ground except on its eastern side, where there is a depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180725_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180725-206">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moons">Moons</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars" title="Moons of Mars">Moons of Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos (moon)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos (moon)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:174px;max-width:174px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:162px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phobos_colour_2008.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3300" data-file-width="3500" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Phobos_colour_2008.jpg/172px-Phobos_colour_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Phobos_colour_2008.jpg/258px-Phobos_colour_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Phobos_colour_2008.jpg/344px-Phobos_colour_2008.jpg 2x" width="172"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Enhanced-color HiRISE image of <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a>, showing a series of mostly parallel grooves and <a href="/wiki/Crater_chain" title="Crater chain">crater chains</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Stickney_(crater)" title="Stickney (crater)">Stickney crater</a> at right</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:162px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deimos-MRO.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="592" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Deimos-MRO.jpg/162px-Deimos-MRO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Deimos-MRO.jpg/243px-Deimos-MRO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Deimos-MRO.jpg/324px-Deimos-MRO.jpg 2x" width="162"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Enhanced-color HiRISE image of <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a> (not to scale), showing its smooth blanket of <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>Mars has two relatively small (compared to Earth's) natural moons, <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> (about 22 km (14 mi) in diameter) and <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a> (about 12 km (7.5 mi) in diameter), which orbit close to the planet. Asteroid capture is a long-favored theory, but their origin remains uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa31031_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa31031-209">[206]</a></sup> Both satellites were discovered in 1877 by <a href="/wiki/Asaph_Hall" title="Asaph Hall">Asaph Hall</a>; they are named after the characters <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(mythology)" title="Phobos (mythology)">Phobos</a> (panic/fear) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deimos_(mythology)" title="Deimos (mythology)">Deimos</a> (terror/dread), who, in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, accompanied their father <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a>, god of war, into battle. Mars was the Roman counterpart of Ares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theoi_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-theoi-210">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qjras19_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-qjras19-211">[208]</a></sup> In modern <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, though, the planet retains its ancient name <i>Ares</i> (Aris: <i>Άρης</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greek_Names_of_the_Planets_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greek_Names_of_the_Planets-212">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the surface of Mars, the motions of Phobos and Deimos appear different from that of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Phobos rises in the west, sets in the east, and rises again in just 11 hours. Deimos, being only just outside <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_orbit" title="Synchronous orbit">synchronous orbit</a> – where the orbital period would match the planet's period of rotation – rises as expected in the east but slowly. Despite the 30-hour orbit of Deimos, 2.7 days elapse between its rise and set for an equatorial observer, as it slowly falls behind the rotation of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phobos.html_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-phobos.html-213">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orbits_of_Phobos_and_Deimos.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="120" data-file-width="160" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/Orbits_of_Phobos_and_Deimos.gif" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Orbits of Phobos and Deimos (to scale)</div></div></div>
<p>Because the orbit of Phobos is below synchronous altitude, the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> from the planet Mars are gradually lowering its orbit. In about 50 million years, it could either crash into Mars's surface or break up into a ring structure around the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phobos.html_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-phobos.html-213">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>The origin of the two moons is not well understood. Their low albedo and <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrite</a> composition have been regarded as similar to asteroids, supporting the capture theory. The unstable orbit of Phobos would seem to point towards a relatively recent capture. But both have <a href="/wiki/Circular_orbit" title="Circular orbit">circular orbits</a>, near the equator, which is unusual for captured objects and the required capture dynamics are complex. Accretion early in the history of Mars is plausible, but would not account for a composition resembling asteroids rather than Mars itself, if that is confirmed.
</p><p>A third possibility is the involvement of a third body or a type of impact disruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ellis07_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-ellis07-214">[211]</a></sup> More-recent lines of evidence for Phobos having a highly porous interior,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andert_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andert-215">[212]</a></sup> and suggesting a composition containing mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllosilicates" title="Phyllosilicates">phyllosilicates</a> and other minerals known from Mars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giuranna_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giuranna-216">[213]</a></sup> point toward an origin of Phobos from material ejected by an impact on Mars that reaccreted in Martian orbit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blast_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blast-217">[214]</a></sup> similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" title="Giant impact hypothesis">prevailing theory</a> for the origin of Earth's moon. Although the <a href="/wiki/VNIR" title="VNIR">VNIR</a> spectra of the moons of Mars resemble those of outer-belt asteroids, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_infrared" title="Thermal infrared">thermal infrared</a> spectra of Phobos are reported to be inconsistent with <a href="/wiki/Chondrite" title="Chondrite">chondrites</a> of any class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giuranna_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-Giuranna-216">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mars may have moons smaller than 50 to 100 metres (160 to 330 ft) in diameter, and a dust ring is predicted to exist between Phobos and Deimos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adler_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-adler-22">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars" title="Exploration of Mars">Exploration of Mars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_(refined).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="496" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_%28refined%29.png/220px-HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_%28refined%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_%28refined%29.png/330px-HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_%28refined%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_%28refined%29.png/440px-HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_%28refined%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HiRISE_image_of_MSL_during_EDL_(refined).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mars Science Laboratory under parachute during its atmospheric entry at Mars</div></div></div>
<p>Dozens of crewless <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Orbiter" title="Orbiter">orbiters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_lander" title="Mars lander">landers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mars_rover" title="Mars rover">rovers</a>, have been sent to Mars by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">United States</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">Europe</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO" title="ISRO">India</a> to study the planet's surface, climate, and geology.
</p><p>As of  2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mars&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Mars is host to eight functioning <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>: six in orbit—<i><a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">2001 Mars Odyssey</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/MAVEN" title="MAVEN">MAVEN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission" title="Mars Orbiter Mission">Mars Orbiter Mission</a> and <a href="/wiki/ExoMars_Trace_Gas_Orbiter" title="ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter">ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter</a>—and two on the surface—<a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rover</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Opportunity_(rover)" title="Opportunity (rover)">Opportunity</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)">Curiosity</a></i>. The public can request images of Mars via the <i>Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> <a href="/wiki/HiWish_program" title="HiWish program">HiWish program</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a>, named <i>Curiosity</i>, launched on November 26, 2011, and reached Mars on August 6, 2012 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>. It is larger and more advanced than the Mars Exploration Rovers, with a movement rate up to 90 m (300 ft) per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-home_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-home-218">[215]</a></sup> Experiments include a laser chemical sampler that can deduce the make-up of rocks at a distance of 7 m (23 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laser_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-laser-219">[216]</a></sup> On February 10, 2013, the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a> obtained the first deep rock samples ever taken from another planetary body, using its on-board drill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[217]</a></sup> The same year, it discovered that Mars's soil contains between 1.5% and 3% water by mass (albeit attached to other compounds and thus not freely accessible).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-221">[218]</a></sup> Observations by the <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a></i> had previously revealed the possibility of flowing water during the warmest months on Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20110804_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20110804-222">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 24, 2014, <a href="/wiki/Mars_Orbiter_Mission" title="Mars Orbiter Mission">Mars Orbiter Mission</a> (MOM), launched by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a>, reached Mars orbit. ISRO launched MOM on November 5, 2013, with the aim of analyzing the Martian atmosphere and topography. The Mars Orbiter Mission used a <a href="/wiki/Hohmann_transfer_orbit" title="Hohmann transfer orbit">Hohmann transfer orbit</a> to escape Earth's gravitational influence and catapult into a nine-month-long voyage to Mars. The mission is the first successful Asian interplanetary mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>, in collaboration with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roscosmos_State_Corporation" title="Roscosmos State Corporation">Roscosmos</a>, launched the <a href="/wiki/ExoMars_Trace_Gas_Orbiter" title="ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter">ExoMars Trace Gas Orbiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schiaparelli_EDM_lander" title="Schiaparelli EDM lander"><i>Schiaparelli</i> lander</a> on March 14, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnews20160314_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews20160314-224">[221]</a></sup> While the Trace Gas Orbiter successfully entered Mars orbit on October 19, 2016, <i>Schiaparelli</i> crashed during its landing attempt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars#Timeline_of_Mars_exploration" title="Exploration of Mars">Exploration of Mars § Timeline of Mars exploration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1013" data-file-width="1800" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg/220px-Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg/330px-Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg/440px-Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orion_docked_to_Mars_Transfer_Vehicle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Concept for a Bimodal Nuclear Thermal Transfer Vehicle in low Earth orbit</div></div></div>
<p>In May 2018 NASA's <i><a href="/wiki/InSight" title="InSight">InSight</a></i> lander was launched, along with the twin <a href="/wiki/Mars_Cube_One" title="Mars Cube One">MarCO</a> <a href="/wiki/CubeSat" title="CubeSat">CubeSats</a> that will fly by Mars and provide a <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> relay for the landing. The mission is expected to arrive at Mars in November 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20160309_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20160309-226">[223]</a></sup> NASA plans to launch its <a href="/wiki/Mars_2020" title="Mars 2020">Mars 2020</a> <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> rover in July or August 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars2020launch_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars2020launch-227">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> will launch the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ExoMars_rover" title="ExoMars rover">ExoMars rover</a> and <a href="/wiki/ExoMars_2020_surface_platform" title="ExoMars 2020 surface platform">surface platform</a> in July 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaunchMoved2020_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaunchMoved2020-228">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Arab Emirates' <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_Hope" title="Mars Hope">Mars Hope</a></i> orbiter is planned for launch in 2020, reaching Mars orbit in 2021. The probe will make a global study of the Martian atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several plans for a <a href="/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars" title="Human mission to Mars">human mission to Mars</a> have been proposed throughout the 20th century and into the 21st century, but no active plan has an arrival date sooner than the 2020s. <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a> presented a plan in September 2016 to, optimistically, launch <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">space tourists</a> to Mars in 2024 at an estimated development cost of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10 billion</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20160927_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20160927-230">[227]</a></sup> In October 2016, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> renewed U.S. policy to pursue the goal of sending humans to Mars in the 2030s, and to continue using the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> as a technology incubator in that pursuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN-20161011_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-20161011-231">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161011_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161011-232">[229]</a></sup> The NASA Authorization Act of 2017 directed NASA to get humans near or on the surface of Mars by the early 2030s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[230]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy_on_Mars">Astronomy on Mars</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_on_Mars" title="Astronomy on Mars">Astronomy on Mars</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Mars" title="Solar eclipses on Mars">Solar eclipses on Mars</a></div>
<p>With the presence of various orbiters, landers, and rovers, it is possible to practice <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> from Mars. Although Mars's moon <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> appears about one-third the <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">full moon</a> on Earth, <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a> appears more or less star-like, looking only slightly brighter than Venus does from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pl_org_deimos_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-pl_org_deimos-234">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various phenomena seen from Earth have also been observed from Mars, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor" title="Meteor">meteors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">auroras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aurora_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-aurora-235">[232]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">apparent sizes</a> of the moons Phobos and Deimos are sufficiently smaller than that of the Sun; thus, their partial "eclipses" of the Sun are best considered <a href="/wiki/Transit_(astronomy)" title="Transit (astronomy)">transits</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Deimos_from_Mars" title="Transit of Deimos from Mars">transit of Deimos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Phobos_from_Mars" title="Transit of Phobos from Mars">Phobos</a> from Mars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature436_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature436-236">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sd040317_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-sd040317-237">[234]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Mercury_from_Mars" title="Transit of Mercury from Mars">Transits of Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">Venus</a> have been observed from Mars. A <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Earth_from_Mars" title="Transit of Earth from Mars">transit of Earth</a> will be seen from Mars on November 10, 2084.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jbaa93_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-jbaa93-238">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 19, 2014, <a href="/wiki/C/2013_A1" title="C/2013 A1">Comet Siding Spring</a> passed extremely close to Mars, so close that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comae" title="Comae">coma</a> may have enveloped Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141019_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141019-239">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141019_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141019-240">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-20141020_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-20141020-241">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISRO_MOM_safe_after_Mars_comet_flyby_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISRO_MOM_safe_after_Mars_comet_flyby-242">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SD-20131201_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-SD-20131201-243">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS-20131206_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-NS-20131206-244">[241]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:791px;max-width:791px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:311px;max-width:311px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:168px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PIA21260_-_Earth_and_Its_Moon%2C_as_Seen_From_Mars.jpg/464px-PIA21260_-_Earth_and_Its_Moon%2C_as_Seen_From_Mars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/PIA21260_-_Earth_and_Its_Moon%2C_as_Seen_From_Mars.jpg/618px-PIA21260_-_Earth_and_Its_Moon%2C_as_Seen_From_Mars.jpg 2x" width="309"/></a></div><div data-file-height="360" data-file-width="360" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/15-ml-06-phobos2-A067R1.jpg/168px-15-ml-06-phobos2-A067R1.jpg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/15-ml-06-phobos2-A067R1.jpg/336px-15-ml-06-phobos2-A067R1.jpg 2x" width="168"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Phobos_from_Mars" title="Transit of Phobos from Mars">transits</a> the Sun (<i><a href="/wiki/Opportunity_(rover)" title="Opportunity (rover)">Opportunity</a></i>, March 10, 2004)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:168px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19801-TrackingSunspotsOnTheSunFromMars-20150708.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="446" data-file-width="801" height="168"
