srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/PIA19801-TrackingSunspotsOnTheSunFromMars-20150708.gif/453px-PIA19801-TrackingSunspotsOnTheSunFromMars-20150708.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/PIA19801-TrackingSunspotsOnTheSunFromMars-20150708.gif/604px-PIA19801-TrackingSunspotsOnTheSunFromMars-20150708.gif 2x" width="302"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Tracking <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a> from Mars</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Viewing">Viewing</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apparent_retrograde_motion_of_Mars_in_2003.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Apparent_retrograde_motion_of_Mars_in_2003.gif/220px-Apparent_retrograde_motion_of_Mars_in_2003.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Apparent_retrograde_motion_of_Mars_in_2003.gif/330px-Apparent_retrograde_motion_of_Mars_in_2003.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/70/Apparent_retrograde_motion_of_Mars_in_2003.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apparent_retrograde_motion_of_Mars_in_2003.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of the apparent retrograde motion of Mars in 2003 as seen from Earth</div></div></div>
<p>The mean <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of Mars is +0.71 with a standard deviation of 1.05.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-12">[10]</a></sup> Because the orbit of Mars is eccentric, the magnitude at <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a> from the Sun can range from about −3.0 to −1.4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MallamaSky_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-MallamaSky-246">[243]</a></sup> The minimum brightness is magnitude +1.86 when the planet is in <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy)" title="Conjunction (astronomy)">conjunction</a> with the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-12">[10]</a></sup> At its brightest, Mars (along with Jupiter) are second only to Venus in luminosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-12">[10]</a></sup> Mars usually appears distinctly yellow, orange, or red. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <i>Spirit</i> rover has taken pictures of a greenish-brown, mud-colored landscape with blue-grey rocks and patches of light red sand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lloyd06_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-lloyd06-247">[244]</a></sup> When farthest away from Earth, it is more than seven times farther away than when it is closest. When least favorably positioned, it can be lost in the Sun's glare for months at a time. At its most favorable times—at 15- or 17-year intervals, and always between late July and late September—a lot of surface detail can be seen with a <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>. Especially noticeable, even at low magnification, are the <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice_cap" title="Polar ice cap">polar ice caps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shallowsky_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-shallowsky-248">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>As Mars approaches opposition, it begins a period of <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">retrograde motion</a>, which means it will appear to move backwards in a looping motion with respect to the background stars. The duration of this retrograde motion lasts for about 72 days, and Mars reaches its peak luminosity in the middle of this motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeilik02_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeilik02-249">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Closest_approaches">Closest approaches</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relative">Relative</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif/220px-Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif/330px-Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif/440px-Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Mars_orbit_around_Earth.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geocentric animation of Mars's orbit relative to Earth from January 2003 to January 2019<br/><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> Mars</span> <b>·</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:RoyalBlue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> Earth</span></div></div></div>
<p>The point at which Mars's geocentric longitude is 180° different from the Sun's is known as <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a>, which is near the time of closest approach to Earth. The time of opposition can occur as much as 8.5 days away from the closest approach. The distance at close approach varies between about 54 and 103 million km (34 and 64 million mi) due to the planets' <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">elliptical</a> orbits, which causes comparable variation in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_size" title="Angular size">angular size</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laskar2003_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laskar2003-250">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa051103_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa051103-251">[248]</a></sup> The last Mars opposition occurred on July 27, 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180801_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180801-252">[249]</a></sup> at a distance of about 58 million km (36 million mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheehan970202_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-sheehan970202-253">[250]</a></sup> The next Mars opposition occurs on October 13, 2020, at a distance of about 63 million km (39 million mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheehan970202_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-sheehan970202-253">[250]</a></sup> The average time between the successive oppositions of Mars, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic_period" title="Synodic period">synodic period</a>, is 780 days; but the number of days between the dates of successive oppositions can range from 764 to 812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astropro_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-astropro-254">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p>As Mars approaches opposition it begins a period of <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">retrograde motion</a>, which makes it appear to move backwards in a looping motion relative to the background stars. The duration of this retrograde motion is about 72 days.
</p>
<h4><span id="Absolute.2C_around_the_present_time"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Absolute,_around_the_present_time">Absolute, around the present time</span></h4>
<p>Mars made its closest approach to Earth and maximum apparent brightness in nearly 60,000 years, 55,758,006 km (0.37271925 AU; 34,646,419 mi), <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">magnitude</a> −2.88, on August 27, 2003, at 9:51:13 UTC. This occurred when Mars was one day from opposition and about three days from its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>, making it particularly easy to see from Earth. The last time it came so close is estimated to have been on September 12, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">57,617 BC</a>, the next time being in 2287.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rao030822_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-rao030822-255">[252]</a></sup> This record approach was only slightly closer than other recent close approaches. For instance, the minimum distance on August 22, 1924, was <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7010557775660904950♠</span>0.37285 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span>, and the minimum distance on August 24, 2208, will be <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7010557685902182530♠</span>0.37279 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meeus2003_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meeus2003-181">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_observations">Historical observations</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mars_observation" title="History of Mars observation">History of Mars observation</a></div>
<p>The history of observations of Mars is marked by the oppositions of Mars, when the planet is closest to Earth and hence is most easily visible, which occur every couple of years. Even more notable are the perihelic oppositions of Mars, which occur every 15 or 17 years and are distinguished because Mars is close to perihelion, making it even closer to Earth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_medieval_observations">Ancient and medieval observations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1180" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/220px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/330px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/440px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, first person to see Mars via telescope in 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jha15_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-jha15-256">[253]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The ancient <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> believed that Mars was <a href="/wiki/Nergal" title="Nergal">Nergal</a>, the god of war and plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabkin2005_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rabkin2005-257">[254]</a></sup> During Sumerian times, Nergal was a minor deity of little significance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabkin2005_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rabkin2005-257">[254]</a></sup> but, during later times, his main cult center was the city of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabkin2005_257-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rabkin2005-257">[254]</a></sup> In Mesopotamian texts, Mars is referred to as the "star of judgement of the fate of the dead".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[255]</a></sup> The existence of Mars as a wandering object in the night sky was recorded by the ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_astronomy" title="Egyptian astronomy">Egyptian astronomers</a> and, by 1534 BCE, they were familiar with the <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">retrograde motion</a> of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paob85_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-paob85-259">[256]</a></sup> By the period of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomers" title="Babylonian astronomers">Babylonian astronomers</a> were making regular records of the positions of the planets and systematic observations of their behavior. For Mars, they knew that the planet made 37 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic_period" title="Synodic period">synodic periods</a>, or 42 circuits of the zodiac, every 79 years. They invented arithmetic methods for making minor corrections to the predicted positions of the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-north08_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-north08-260">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swerdlow98_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-swerdlow98-261">[258]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the fourth century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> noted that Mars disappeared behind the Moon during an <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a>, indicating that the planet was farther away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poor08_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-poor08-262">[259]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, a Greek living in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-google-263">[260]</a></sup> attempted to address the problem of the orbital motion of Mars. Ptolemy's model and his collective work on astronomy was presented in the multi-volume collection <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>, which became the authoritative treatise on <a href="/wiki/History_of_astronomy#Medieval_Western_Europe" title="History of astronomy">Western astronomy</a> for the next fourteen centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google7_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-google7-264">[261]</a></sup> Literature from ancient China confirms that Mars was known by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomers</a> by no later than the fourth century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_ronan85_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_ronan85-265">[262]</a></sup> In the fifth century CE, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">Indian astronomical</a> text <i><a href="/wiki/Surya_Siddhanta" title="Surya Siddhanta">Surya Siddhanta</a></i> estimated the diameter of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jse97_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-jse97-266">[263]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian" title="East Asian">East Asian</a> cultures, Mars is traditionally referred to as the "fire star" (Chinese: <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">火星</span>), based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_elements_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Five elements (Chinese philosophy)">Five elements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the seventeenth century, <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> measured the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diurnal_parallax" title="Diurnal parallax">diurnal parallax</a> of Mars that <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> used to make a preliminary calculation of the relative distance to the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taton03_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-taton03-270">[267]</a></sup> When the telescope became available, the diurnal parallax of Mars was again measured in an effort to determine the Sun-Earth distance. This was first performed by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Domenico Cassini</a> in 1672. The early parallax measurements were hampered by the quality of the instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hirschfeld01_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-hirschfeld01-271">[268]</a></sup> The only <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> of Mars by Venus observed was that of October 13, 1590, seen by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Maestlin" title="Michael Maestlin">Michael Maestlin</a> at <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg" title="Heidelberg">Heidelberg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sat57_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-sat57-272">[269]</a></sup> In 1610, Mars was viewed by <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, who was first to see it via telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jha15_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-jha15-256">[253]</a></sup> The first person to draw a map of Mars that displayed any terrain features was the Dutch astronomer <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arizona_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-arizona-273">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Martian_.22canals.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Martian_"canals"'>Martian "canals"</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="713" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png/200px-Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png/300px-Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png/400px-Karte_Mars_Schiaparelli_MKL1888.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Map of Mars by Giovanni Schiaparelli</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="409" data-file-width="800" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg/200px-Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg/300px-Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg/400px-Lowell_Mars_channels.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Mars sketched as observed by Lowell before 1914 (south on top)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_HST_Mollweide_map_1999.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="900" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Mars_HST_Mollweide_map_1999.png/200px-Mars_HST_Mollweide_map_1999.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Mars_HST_Mollweide_map_1999.png/300px-Mars_HST_Mollweide_map_1999.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Mars_HST_Mollweide_map_1999.png/400px-Mars_HST_Mollweide_map_1999.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Map of Mars from the <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> as seen near the 1999 opposition (north on top)</div></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Martian_canal" title="Martian canal">Martian canal</a></div>
<p>By the 19th century, the resolution of telescopes reached a level sufficient for surface features to be identified. A perihelic opposition of Mars occurred on September 5, 1877. In that year, the Italian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Schiaparelli" title="Giovanni Schiaparelli">Giovanni Schiaparelli</a> used a 22 cm (8.7 in) telescope in <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> to help produce the first detailed map of Mars. These maps notably contained features he called <i>canali</i>, which were later shown to be an <a href="/wiki/Optical_illusion" title="Optical illusion">optical illusion</a>. These <i>canali</i> were supposedly long, straight lines on the surface of Mars, to which he gave names of famous rivers on Earth. His term, which means "channels" or "grooves", was popularly mistranslated in English as "canals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snyder01_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-snyder01-274">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sagan80_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-sagan80-275">[272]</a></sup>
</p><p>Influenced by the observations, the orientalist <a href="/wiki/Percival_Lowell" title="Percival Lowell">Percival Lowell</a> founded an <a href="/wiki/Lowell_Observatory" title="Lowell Observatory">observatory</a> which had 30 and 45 cm (12 and 18 in) telescopes. The observatory was used for the exploration of Mars during the last good opportunity in 1894 and the following less favorable oppositions. He published several books on Mars and life on the planet, which had a great influence on the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basalla06_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-basalla06-276">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151001_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151001-277">[274]</a></sup> The <i>canali</i> were independently found by other astronomers, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henri_Joseph_Perrotin" title="Henri Joseph Perrotin">Henri Joseph Perrotin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Thollon" title="Louis Thollon">Louis Thollon</a> in Nice, using one of the largest telescopes of that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maria_lane05_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-maria_lane05-278">[275]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ba3_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-ba3-279">[276]</a></sup>
</p><p>The seasonal changes (consisting of the diminishing of the polar caps and the dark areas formed during Martian summer) in combination with the canals led to speculation about life on Mars, and it was a long-held belief that Mars contained vast seas and vegetation. The telescope never reached the resolution required to give proof to any speculations. As bigger telescopes were used, fewer long, straight <i>canali</i> were observed. During an observation in 1909 by <a href="/wiki/Camille_Flammarion" title="Camille Flammarion">Flammarion</a> with an 84 cm (33 in) telescope, irregular patterns were observed, but no <i>canali</i> were seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zahnle01_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-zahnle01-280">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even in the 1960s articles were published on Martian biology, putting aside explanations other than life for the seasonal changes on Mars. Detailed scenarios for the metabolism and chemical cycles for a functional ecosystem have been published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science136_3510_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-science136_3510-281">[278]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_visitation">Spacecraft visitation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars" title="Exploration of Mars">Exploration of Mars</a></div>
<p>Once <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> visited the planet during NASA's <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner missions</a> in the 1960s and 70s, these concepts were radically broken. The results of the Viking life-detection experiments aided an intermission in which the hypothesis of a hostile, dead planet was generally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ward_brownlee00_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-ward_brownlee00-282">[279]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mariner 9 and Viking allowed better maps of Mars to be made using the data from these missions, and another major leap forward was the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a> mission, launched in 1996 and operated until late 2006, that allowed complete, extremely detailed maps of the Martian topography, magnetic field and surface minerals to be obtained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distant_worlds:_milestones_in_planetary_exploration_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Distant_worlds:_milestones_in_planetary_exploration-283">[280]</a></sup> These maps are available online; for example, at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_Mars" title="Google Mars">Google Mars</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a> continued exploring with new instruments, and supporting lander missions. NASA provides two online tools: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mars_Trek&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mars Trek (page does not exist)">Mars Trek</a>, which provides visualizations of the planet using data from 50 years of exploration, and <a href="/wiki/Experience_Curiosity" title="Experience Curiosity">Experience Curiosity</a>, which simulates traveling on Mars in 3-D with Curiosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[281]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mars_in_culture" title="Mars in culture">Mars in culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars_in_fiction" title="Mars in fiction">Mars in fiction</a></div>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_symbol.svg"><img alt="Mars symbol.svg" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mars_symbol.svg/70px-Mars_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mars_symbol.svg/105px-Mars_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mars_symbol.svg/140px-Mars_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="70"/></a></div>
<p>Mars is named after the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">god of war</a>. In different cultures, Mars represents masculinity and youth. <a href="/wiki/Gender_symbol" title="Gender symbol">Its symbol</a>, a circle with an arrow pointing out to the upper right, is used as a symbol for the male gender.
</p><p>The many failures in Mars exploration probes resulted in a satirical counter-culture blaming the failures on an Earth-Mars "<a href="/wiki/Bermuda_Triangle" title="Bermuda Triangle">Bermuda Triangle</a>", a "<a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars#Mars_Curse" title="Exploration of Mars">Mars Curse</a>", or a "Great Galactic Ghoul" that feeds on Martian spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinerman04_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinerman04-285">[282]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Intelligent_.22Martians.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Intelligent_"Martians"'>Intelligent "Martians"</span></h3>
<p>The fashionable idea that Mars was populated by intelligent <a href="/wiki/Martian" title="Martian">Martians</a> exploded in the late 19th century. <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Schiaparelli" title="Giovanni Schiaparelli">Schiaparelli's</a> "canali" observations combined with <a href="/wiki/Percival_Lowell" title="Percival Lowell">Percival Lowell</a>'s books on the subject put forward the standard notion of a planet that was a drying, cooling, dying world with ancient civilizations constructing irrigation works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prion_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-prion-286">[283]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="858" data-file-width="713" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg/170px-Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg/255px-Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg/340px-Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kirks_Soap_Yerkes_Mars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1893 soap ad playing on the popular idea that Mars was populated</div></div></div>
<p>Many other observations and proclamations by notable personalities added to what has been termed "Mars Fever".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fergus04_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-fergus04-287">[284]</a></sup> In 1899, while investigating atmospheric radio noise using his receivers in his Colorado Springs lab, inventor <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a> observed repetitive signals that he later surmised might have been radio communications coming from another planet, possibly Mars. In a 1901 interview Tesla said:
</p>
<blockquote><p>It was some time afterward when the thought flashed upon my mind that the disturbances I had observed might be due to an intelligent control. Although I could not decipher their meaning, it was impossible for me to think of them as having been entirely accidental. The feeling is constantly growing on me that I had been the first to hear the greeting of one planet to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tesla01_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-tesla01-288">[285]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Tesla's theories gained support from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Kelvin" title="Lord Kelvin">Lord Kelvin</a> who, while visiting the United States in 1902, was reported to have said that he thought Tesla had picked up Martian signals being sent to the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheney81_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-cheney81-289">[286]</a></sup> Kelvin "emphatically" denied this report shortly before leaving: "What I really said was that the inhabitants of Mars, if there are any, were doubtless able to see New York, particularly the glare of the electricity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt020511_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt020511-290">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> article in 1901, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Charles_Pickering" title="Edward Charles Pickering">Edward Charles Pickering</a>, director of the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College_Observatory" title="Harvard College Observatory">Harvard College Observatory</a>, said that they had received a telegram from <a href="/wiki/Lowell_Observatory" title="Lowell Observatory">Lowell Observatory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> that seemed to confirm that Mars was trying to communicate with Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt2_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt2-291">[288]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Early in December 1900, we received from Lowell Observatory in Arizona a telegram that a shaft of light had been seen to project from Mars (the Lowell observatory makes a specialty of Mars) lasting seventy minutes. I wired these facts to Europe and sent out neostyle copies through this country. The observer there is a careful, reliable man and there is no reason to doubt that the light existed. It was given as from a well-known geographical point on Mars. That was all. Now the story has gone the world over. In Europe it is stated that I have been in communication with Mars, and all sorts of exaggerations have spring up. Whatever the light was, we have no means of knowing. Whether it had intelligence or not, no one can say. It is absolutely inexplicable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt2_291-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt2-291">[288]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Pickering later proposed creating a set of mirrors in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, intended to signal Martians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fradin99_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-fradin99-292">[289]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:War-of-the-worlds-tripod.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="334" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/War-of-the-worlds-tripod.jpg/170px-War-of-the-worlds-tripod.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/War-of-the-worlds-tripod.jpg/255px-War-of-the-worlds-tripod.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5a/War-of-the-worlds-tripod.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:War-of-the-worlds-tripod.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Martian tripod illustration from the 1906 French edition of <i>The War of the Worlds</i> by H. G. Wells</div></div></div>
<p>In recent decades, the high-resolution mapping of the surface of Mars, culminating in <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">Mars Global Surveyor</a>, revealed no artifacts of habitation by "intelligent" life, but pseudoscientific speculation about intelligent life on Mars continues from commentators such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._Hoagland" title="Richard C. Hoagland">Richard C. Hoagland</a>. Reminiscent of the <i>canali</i> controversy, these speculations are based on small scale features perceived in the spacecraft images, such as "pyramids" and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Face_on_Mars" title="Face on Mars">Face on Mars</a>". Planetary astronomer <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Mars has become a kind of mythic arena onto which we have projected our Earthly hopes and fears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sagan80_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-sagan80-275">[272]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The depiction of Mars in fiction has been stimulated by its dramatic red color and by nineteenth century scientific speculations that its surface conditions might support not just life but intelligent life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lightman97_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-lightman97-293">[290]</a></sup> Thus originated a large number of <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> scenarios, among which is <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_Worlds" title="The War of the Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a></i>, published in 1898, in which Martians seek to escape their dying planet by invading Earth.
</p><p>Influential works included <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">Ray Bradbury</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Martian_Chronicles" title="The Martian Chronicles">The Martian Chronicles</a></i>, in which human explorers accidentally destroy a Martian civilization, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Rice_Burroughs" title="Edgar Rice Burroughs">Edgar Rice Burroughs</a>' <a href="/wiki/Barsoom" title="Barsoom"><i>Barsoom</i> series</a>, <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a>' novel <i><a href="/wiki/Out_of_the_Silent_Planet" title="Out of the Silent Planet">Out of the Silent Planet</a></i> (1938),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanford09_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-sanford09-294">[291]</a></sup> and a number of <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Heinlein" title="Robert A. Heinlein">Robert A. Heinlein</a> stories before the mid-sixties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buker02_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-buker02-295">[292]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a> made reference to the moons of Mars, about 150 years before their actual discovery by <a href="/wiki/Asaph_Hall" title="Asaph Hall">Asaph Hall</a>, detailing reasonably accurate descriptions of their orbits, in the 19th chapter of his novel <i><a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver's Travels">Gulliver's Travels</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jonathan_swift_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-jonathan_swift-296">[293]</a></sup>
</p><p>A comic figure of an intelligent Martian, <a href="/wiki/Marvin_the_Martian" title="Marvin the Martian">Marvin the Martian</a>, appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/Haredevil_Hare" title="Haredevil Hare">Haredevil Hare</a></i> (1948) as a character in the <a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes" title="Looney Tunes">Looney Tunes</a> <a href="/wiki/Animated_cartoon" title="Animated cartoon">animated cartoons</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warner_Brothers" title="Warner Brothers">Warner Brothers</a>, and has continued as part of popular culture to the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rabkin05_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-rabkin05-297">[294]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">Viking</a> spacecraft had returned pictures of Mars as it really is, an apparently lifeless and canal-less world, these ideas about Mars had to be abandoned, and a vogue for accurate, realist depictions of human colonies on Mars developed, the best known of which may be <a href="/wiki/Kim_Stanley_Robinson" title="Kim Stanley Robinson">Kim Stanley Robinson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mars_trilogy" title="Mars trilogy"><i>Mars</i> trilogy</a>. Pseudo-scientific speculations about the Face on Mars and other enigmatic landmarks spotted by <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probes</a> have meant that ancient civilizations continue to be a popular theme in science fiction, especially in film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miles_peters_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-miles_peters-298">[295]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
</div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This image was taken by the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)"><i>Rosetta</i></a> spacecraft's <a href="/wiki/Optical,_Spectroscopic,_and_Infrared_Remote_Imaging_System" title="Optical, Spectroscopic, and Infrared Remote Imaging System">Optical, Spectroscopic, and Infrared Remote Imaging System</a> (OSIRIS), at a distance of ≈240,000 kilometres (150,000 mi) during its February 2007 encounter. The view is centered on the <a href="/wiki/Aeolis_quadrangle" title="Aeolis quadrangle">Aeolis quadrangle</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Gale_(crater)" title="Gale (crater)">Gale crater</a>, the landing site of the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)"><i>Curiosity</i> rover</a>, prominently visible just left of center. The darker, more heavily cratered terrain in the south, <a href="/wiki/Terra_Cimmeria" title="Terra Cimmeria">Terra Cimmeria</a>, is composed of older terrain than the much smoother and brighter <a href="/wiki/Elysium_Planitia" title="Elysium Planitia">Elysium Planitia</a> to the north. Geologically recent processes, such as the possible existence of a <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">global ocean</a> in Mars's past, could have helped lower-elevated areas, such as Elysium Planitia, retain a more youthful look.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-best-fit_ellipsoid-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-best-fit_ellipsoid_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-best-fit_ellipsoid_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-best-fit_ellipsoid_4-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"> Best-fit <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-serpentinization-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-serpentinization_154-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> There are many <i><a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">serpentinization</a></i> reactions. <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">Olivine</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Solid_solution" title="Solid solution">solid solution</a> between <a href="/wiki/Forsterite" title="Forsterite">forsterite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fayalite" title="Fayalite">fayalite</a> whose general formula is (Fe,Mg)<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>. The reaction producing methane from olivine can be written as: <i>Forsterite + Fayalite + Water + Carbonic acid → Serpentine + Magnetite + Methane</i>, or (in balanced form): 18Mg<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span> + 6Fe<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span> + 26H<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>O + CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>  →  12Mg<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>3</span>Si<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>O<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>5</span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span> + 4Fe<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>3</span>O<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span> + CH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
